<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Decolonization</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Undoing political, economic and cultural legacies of colonisation</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the undoing of colonialism. For medical interventions, see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decolonisation_(medicine)" title="Decolonisation (medicine)">Decolonisation (medicine)</a>.</div>
<p><b>Decolonization</b> (<a href="/wiki/American_English" title="American English">American</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canadian_English" title="Canadian English">Canadian</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxford_English" title="Oxford English">Oxford English</a>) or <b>decolonisation</b> (other <a href="/wiki/British_English" title="British English">British English</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commonwealth_English" title="Commonwealth English">Commonwealth English</a>) is the undoing of <a href="/wiki/Colonialism" title="Colonialism">colonialism</a>, the latter being the process whereby imperial nations establish and dominate foreign territories, often overseas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> Some scholars of decolonization focus especially on <a href="/wiki/Independence" title="Independence">independence movements</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colonies" title="Colonies">colonies</a> and the collapse of global <a href="/wiki/Colonial_empire" title="Colonial empire">colonial empires</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Other scholars extend the meaning to include economic, cultural and psychological aspects of the colonial experience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-:6-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Decoloniality" title="Decoloniality">Decolonisation scholars</a> apply the framework to struggles against <a href="/wiki/Coloniality_of_power" title="Coloniality of power">coloniality of power</a> within <a href="/wiki/Settler_colonialism" title="Settler colonialism">settler-colonial</a> states even after successful independence movements. <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_decolonization" title="Indigenous decolonization">Indigenous</a> and <a href="/wiki/Postcolonialism" title="Postcolonialism">post-colonial</a> scholars have critiqued Western worldviews, promoting <a href="/wiki/Decolonization_of_knowledge" title="Decolonization of knowledge">decolonization of knowledge</a> and the centering of <a href="/wiki/Traditional_ecological_knowledge" title="Traditional ecological knowledge">traditional ecological knowledge</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-:5-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style><div class="toclimit-3"><div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Scope">Scope</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decolonization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Scope">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> (UN) states that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_right" title="Human right">fundamental right</a> to <a href="/wiki/Self-determination" title="Self-determination">self-determination</a> is the core requirement for decolonization, and that this right can be exercised with or without political independence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Declaration_on_the_Granting_of_Independence_to_Colonial_Countries_and_Peoples" title="Declaration on the Granting of Independence to Colonial Countries and Peoples">UN General Assembly Resolution in 1960</a> characterised colonial foreign rule as a violation of human rights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-:02-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-UN-9">[9]</a></sup> In states that have won independence, <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_decolonization" title="Indigenous decolonization">Indigenous people</a> living under <a href="/wiki/Settler_colonialism" title="Settler colonialism">settler colonialism</a> continue to make demands for decolonization and self-determination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although examples of decolonization can be found as early as the writings of <a href="/wiki/Thucydides" title="Thucydides">Thucydides</a>,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> there have been several particularly active periods of decolonization in modern times. These include the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_American_wars_of_independence" title="Spanish American wars of independence">breakup of the Spanish Empire</a> in the 19th century; of the <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">German</a>, <a href="/wiki/Austria-Hungary" title="Austria-Hungary">Austro-Hungarian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian</a> empires following <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>; of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British</a>, <a href="/wiki/French_colonial_empire" title="French colonial empire">French</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dutch_colonial_empire" title="Dutch colonial empire">Dutch</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Portuguese_colonial_empire" title="Portuguese colonial empire">Portuguese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belgian_colonial_empire" title="Belgian colonial empire">Belgian</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_colonial_empire" title="Italian colonial empire">Italian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_colonial_empire" title="Japanese colonial empire">Japanese</a> colonial empires following <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>; and of the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> at the end of the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>Early studies of decolonisation appeared in the 1960s and 1970s. An important book from this period was <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wretched_of_the_Earth" title="The Wretched of the Earth">the wretched of the earth</a></i> (1961) by Martiniquan author <a href="/wiki/Frantz_Fanon" title="Frantz Fanon">Frantz Fanon</a>, which established many aspects of decolonisation that would be considered in later works. Subsequent studies of decolonisation addressed economic disparities as a legacy of colonialism as well as the annihilation of people's cultures. <a href="/wiki/Ng%C5%A9g%C4%A9_wa_Thiong%27o" title="Ngũgĩ wa Thiong'o">Ngũgĩ wa Thiong'o</a> explored the cultural and linguistic legacies of colonialism in the influential book <i><a href="/wiki/Decolonising_the_Mind" title="Decolonising the Mind">Decolonising the Mind</a></i> (1986).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-:6-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>Decolonization has been used to refer to the <a href="/wiki/Decolonization_of_knowledge" title="Decolonization of knowledge">intellectual decolonization</a> from the colonizers' ideas that made the colonized feel inferior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Issues of decolonization persist and are raised contemporarily. In <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin America</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>, such issues are increasingly discussed under the term <a href="/wiki/Decoloniality" title="Decoloniality">decoloniality</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Methods_and_stages">Methods and stages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decolonization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Methods and stages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gungu_la_mcezo_contre_la_France_%C3%A0_Mayotte.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2165" data-file-width="3388" decoding="async" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Gungu_la_mcezo_contre_la_France_%C3%A0_Mayotte.jpg/220px-Gungu_la_mcezo_contre_la_France_%C3%A0_Mayotte.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Gungu_la_mcezo_contre_la_France_%C3%A0_Mayotte.jpg/330px-Gungu_la_mcezo_contre_la_France_%C3%A0_Mayotte.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Gungu_la_mcezo_contre_la_France_%C3%A0_Mayotte.jpg/440px-Gungu_la_mcezo_contre_la_France_%C3%A0_Mayotte.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gungu_la_mcezo_contre_la_France_%C3%A0_Mayotte.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Comorians protest against <a href="/wiki/2009_Mahoran_status_referendum" title="2009 Mahoran status referendum">Mayotte referendum</a> on becoming an overseas department of France, 2009</div></div></div>
<p>A momentum for decolonization followed <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, in particular through the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a> for European territories that were under the control of the Ottoman and Austria-Hungarian empires. Under Article 22 of the Covenant of the League of Nations, a number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/League_of_Nations_Mandate" title="League of Nations Mandate">mandates</a> were created. The expressed intention was to prepare these countries for self-government, but the mandates are often interpreted as a mere redistribution of control over non-European former colonies of the defeated powers, mainly the <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">German Empire</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>. This reassignment work continued through the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>, with a similar system of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_Nations_Trust_Territories" title="United Nations Trust Territories">trust territories</a> created to adjust control over both former colonies and mandated territories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>The United Nations' 1960 <a href="/wiki/Declaration_on_the_Granting_of_Independence_to_Colonial_Countries_and_Peoples" title="Declaration on the Granting of Independence to Colonial Countries and Peoples">Declaration on the Granting of Independence to Colonial Countries and Peoples</a> was a crucial landmark in decolonization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Theories_for_trends_in_decolonization">Theories for trends in decolonization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decolonization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Theories for trends in decolonization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Scholars have proposed various explanations for trends in decolonization. Two prominent explanations emphasize the state of the world economy (such as whether there is <a href="/wiki/Hegemony" title="Hegemony">hegemony</a> and economic growth or stagnation) and <a href="/wiki/World_polity_theory" title="World polity theory">World Polity theory</a> (which emphasizes the diffusion of the nation-state model from Western Europe to its dependencies and contagion processes whereby decolonization in one instance prompts subsequent decolonizations in other cases).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-18">[18]</a></sup> Other explanations emphasize how the lower profitability of colonization and the costs associated with empire prompted decolonization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Some explanations emphasize how colonial powers struggled militarily against insurgents in the colonies due to a shift from 19th century conditions of "strong political will, a permissive international environment, access to local collaborators, and flexibility to pick their battles" to 20th century conditions of "apathetic publics, hostile superpowers, vanishing collaborators, and constrained options."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Independence_movements">Independence movements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decolonization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Independence movements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Beginning with the emergence of the United States in the 1770s, decolonization took place in the context of <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_history" title="Atlantic history">Atlantic history</a>, against the background of the American and French revolutions. Decolonization became a wider movement in many colonies in the 20th century, and a reality after 1945.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>The historian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Hardy_McNeill" title="William Hardy McNeill">William Hardy McNeill</a>, in his famous 1963 book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Rise_of_the_West" title="The Rise of the West">The Rise of the West</a></i>, appears to have interpreted the post-1945 decline of European empires as paradoxically being due to <a href="/wiki/Westernization" title="Westernization">Westernization</a> itself, writing that
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Although European empires have decayed since 1945, and the separate nation-states of Europe have been eclipsed as centres of political power by the melding of peoples and nations occurring under the aegis of both the American and Russian governments, it remains true that, since the end of World War II, the scramble to imitate and appropriate science, technology, and other aspects of Western culture has accelerated enormously all round the world. Thus the dethronement of western Europe from its brief mastery of the globe coincided with (and was caused by) an unprecedented, rapid Westernization of all the peoples of the earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McNeill_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-McNeill-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 566">: 566 </span></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>In the same book, McNeill wrote that "The rise of the West, as intended by the title and meaning of this book, is only accelerated when one or another Asian or African people throws off European administration by making Western techniques, attitudes, and ideas sufficiently their own to permit them to do so".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McNeill_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-McNeill-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 807">: 807 </span></sup>
</p><p>Great Britain's <a href="/wiki/Thirteen_Colonies" title="Thirteen Colonies">Thirteen North American colonies</a> were the first colonies to break from their colonial motherland by declaring independence as the United States of America in 1776, and being recognized as an independent nation by France in 1778 and Britain in 1783.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Haitian_Revolution">Haitian Revolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decolonization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Haitian Revolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Haitian_Revolution" title="Haitian Revolution">Haitian Revolution</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Haitian_Revolution" title="Haitian Revolution">Haitian Revolution</a> was a revolt in 1789 and subsequent slave uprising in 1791 in the French colony of <a href="/wiki/Saint-Domingue" title="Saint-Domingue">Saint-Domingue</a>, on the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Sea" title="Caribbean Sea">Caribbean</a> island of <a href="/wiki/Hispaniola" title="Hispaniola">Hispaniola</a>. In 1804, <a href="/wiki/Haiti" title="Haiti">Haiti</a> secured independence from France as the <a href="/wiki/First_Empire_of_Haiti" title="First Empire of Haiti">Empire of Haiti</a>, which later became a republic.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spanish_America">Spanish America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decolonization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Spanish America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Spanish_American_wars_of_independence" title="Spanish American wars of independence">Spanish American wars of independence</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:JuraIndependencia.jpg"><img alt="Portrait of the Chilean declaration of independence" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="709" data-file-width="1077" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/JuraIndependencia.jpg/220px-JuraIndependencia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/JuraIndependencia.jpg/330px-JuraIndependencia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/JuraIndependencia.jpg/440px-JuraIndependencia.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:JuraIndependencia.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Chilean_Declaration_of_Independence" title="Chilean Declaration of Independence">Chilean Declaration of Independence</a> on 18 February 1818</div></div></div>
<p>The chaos of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleonic_wars" title="Napoleonic wars">Napoleonic wars</a> in Europe cut the direct links between Spain and its American colonies, allowing for the process of decolonization to begin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>With the invasion of Spain by Napoleon in 1806, the American colonies declared autonomy and loyalty to King Ferdinand VII. The contract was broken and the regions of the Spanish Empire had to decide whether to show allegiance to the Junta of Cadiz (the only territory in Spain free from Napoleon) or have a junta (assembly) of its own. The economic monopoly of the metropolis was the main reason why many countries decided to become independent from Spain. In 1809, the independence wars of Latin America began with a revolt in La Paz, Bolivia. In 1807 and 1808, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viceroyalty_of_the_River_Plate" title="Viceroyalty of the River Plate">Viceroyalty of the River Plate</a> was invaded by the British. After their 2nd defeat, a Frenchman called Santiague de Liniers was proclaimed a new Viceroy by the local population and later accepted by Spain. In May 1810 in <a href="/wiki/Buenos_Aires" title="Buenos Aires">Buenos Aires</a>, a Junta was created, but in <a href="/wiki/Montevideo" title="Montevideo">Montevideo</a> it was not recognized by the local government who followed the authority of the Junta of Cadiz. The rivalry between the two cities was the main reason for the distrust between them. During the next 15 years, the Spanish and Royalist on one side, and the rebels on the other fought in South America and Mexico. Numerous countries declared their independence. In 1824, the Spanish forces were defeated in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ayacucho" title="Battle of Ayacucho">Battle of Ayacucho</a>. The mainland was free, and in 1898, Spain lost <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a> and <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Spanish%E2%80%93American_War" title="Spanish–American War">Spanish–American War</a>. Puerto Rico became an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unincorporated_territory" title="Unincorporated territory">unincorporated territory</a> of the US, but Cuba became independent in 1902.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Portuguese_America">Portuguese America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decolonization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Portuguese America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Independence_of_Brazil" title="Independence of Brazil">Independence of Brazil</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Independencia_brasil_001.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3040" data-file-width="4840" decoding="async" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Independencia_brasil_001.jpg/220px-Independencia_brasil_001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Independencia_brasil_001.jpg/330px-Independencia_brasil_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Independencia_brasil_001.jpg/440px-Independencia_brasil_001.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Independencia_brasil_001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Pedro_I_of_Brazil" title="Pedro I of Brazil">Prince Pedro</a> proclaims himself Emperor of an independent Brazil on 7 September 1822</div></div></div>
<p>The Napoleonic Wars also led to the severing of the direct links between Portugal and its only American colony, <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>. Days before Napoleon invaded Portugal, in 1807 the Portuguese royal court <a href="/wiki/Transfer_of_the_Portuguese_court_to_Brazil" title="Transfer of the Portuguese court to Brazil">fled to Brazil</a>. In 1820 there was a <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Revolution_of_1820" title="Liberal Revolution of 1820">Constitutionalist Revolution</a> in Portugal, which led to the return of the Portuguese court to Lisbon. This led to distrust between the Portuguese and the Brazilian colonists, and finally, in 1822, to the colony becoming independent as the <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Brazil" title="Empire of Brazil">Empire of Brazil</a>, which later became a republic.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="British_Empire">British Empire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decolonization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: British Empire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a></div>
<p>The emergence of Indigenous political parties was especially characteristic of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>, which seemed less ruthless than, for example, Belgium, in controlling political dissent. Driven by pragmatic demands of budgets and manpower the British made deals with the local politicians. Across the empire, the general protocol was to convene a constitutional conference in London to discuss the transition to greater self-government and then independence, submit a report of the constitutional conference to parliament, if approved submit a bill to Parliament at Westminster to terminate the responsibility of the United Kingdom (with a copy of the new constitution annexed), and finally, if approved, issuance of an Order of Council fixing the exact date of independence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>After <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, several former German and Ottoman territories in the Middle East, Africa, and the Pacific were governed by the UK as <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations_mandate" title="League of Nations mandate">League of Nations mandates</a>. Some were administered directly by the UK, and others by British dominions – <a href="/wiki/Nauru" title="Nauru">Nauru</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Territory_of_New_Guinea" title="Territory of New Guinea">Territory of New Guinea</a> by <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_West_Africa" title="South West Africa">South West Africa</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Union_of_South_Africa" title="Union of South Africa">Union of South Africa</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Samoa" title="Western Samoa">Western Samoa</a> by <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_peacemakers-_George_Gavan_Duffy,_Erskine_Childers,_Robert_Barton_and_Arthur_Griffith_in_a_group_(28455606301).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1459" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/The_peacemakers-_George_Gavan_Duffy%2C_Erskine_Childers%2C_Robert_Barton_and_Arthur_Griffith_in_a_group_%2828455606301%29.jpg/220px-The_peacemakers-_George_Gavan_Duffy%2C_Erskine_Childers%2C_Robert_Barton_and_Arthur_Griffith_in_a_group_%2828455606301%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/The_peacemakers-_George_Gavan_Duffy%2C_Erskine_Childers%2C_Robert_Barton_and_Arthur_Griffith_in_a_group_%2828455606301%29.jpg/330px-The_peacemakers-_George_Gavan_Duffy%2C_Erskine_Childers%2C_Robert_Barton_and_Arthur_Griffith_in_a_group_%2828455606301%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/The_peacemakers-_George_Gavan_Duffy%2C_Erskine_Childers%2C_Robert_Barton_and_Arthur_Griffith_in_a_group_%2828455606301%29.jpg/440px-The_peacemakers-_George_Gavan_Duffy%2C_Erskine_Childers%2C_Robert_Barton_and_Arthur_Griffith_in_a_group_%2828455606301%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_peacemakers-_George_Gavan_Duffy,_Erskine_Childers,_Robert_Barton_and_Arthur_Griffith_in_a_group_(28455606301).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Members of the Irish delegation for the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Irish_Treaty" title="Anglo-Irish Treaty">Anglo-Irish Treaty</a> negotiations in December 1921</div></div></div>
<p>Egypt became independent in 1922, although the UK retained security prerogatives, control of the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal" title="Suez Canal">Suez Canal</a>, and effective control of the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Egyptian_Sudan" title="Anglo-Egyptian Sudan">Anglo-Egyptian Sudan</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Balfour_Declaration_of_1926" title="Balfour Declaration of 1926">Balfour Declaration of 1926</a> declared the British Empire <a href="/wiki/Dominion" title="Dominion">dominions</a> as equals, and the 1931 <a href="/wiki/Statute_of_Westminster_1931" title="Statute of Westminster 1931">Statute of Westminster</a> established full legislative independence for them. The equal dominions were six– <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newfoundland" title="Newfoundland">Newfoundland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Free_State" title="Irish Free State">Irish Free State</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Union_of_South_Africa" title="Union of South Africa">Union of South Africa</a>; Ireland had been brought into a union with Great Britain in 1801 creating The United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland until the formation of the Irish Free State in 1922. However, some of the Dominions were already independent de facto, and even de jure and recognized as such by the international community. Thus, Canada was a founding member of the League of Nations in 1919 and served on the council from 1927 to 1930.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> That country also negotiated on its own and signed bilateral and multilateral treaties and conventions from the early 1900s onward. Newfoundland ceded self-rule back to London in 1934. <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>, a League of Nations mandate, became independent in 1932.
</p><p>In response to a growing <a href="/wiki/Indian_independence_movement" title="Indian independence movement">Indian independence movement</a>, the UK made successive reforms to the <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">British Raj</a>, culminating in the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_India_Act_1935" title="Government of India Act 1935">Government of India Act (1935)</a>. These reforms included creating elected legislative councils in some of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Provinces_of_British_India" title="Provinces of British India">Provinces of British India</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mohandas_Karamchand_Gandhi" title="Mohandas Karamchand Gandhi">Mohandas Karamchand Gandhi</a>, India's independence movement leader, led a peaceful resistance to British rule. By becoming a symbol of both peace and opposition to British <a href="/wiki/Imperialism" title="Imperialism">imperialism</a>, many Indians began to view the British as the cause of India's problems leading to a newfound sense of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nationalist_Movements_in_India" title="Nationalist Movements in India">nationalism</a> among its population. With this new wave of Indian nationalism, Gandhi was eventually able to garner the support needed to push back the British and create an independent India in 1947.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:British_Empire_in_February_1952.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="623" data-file-width="1423" decoding="async" height="105" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/British_Empire_in_February_1952.png/240px-British_Empire_in_February_1952.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/British_Empire_in_February_1952.png/360px-British_Empire_in_February_1952.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/British_Empire_in_February_1952.png/480px-British_Empire_in_February_1952.png 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:British_Empire_in_February_1952.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>British Empire in 1952</div></div></div>
<p>Africa was only fully drawn into the colonial system at the end of the 19th century. In the north-east the continued independence of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Empire_of_Ethiopia" title="Empire of Ethiopia">Empire of Ethiopia</a> remained a beacon of hope to pro-independence activists. However, with the anti-colonial wars of the 1900s (decade) barely over, new modernizing forms of <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">African</a> Nationalism began to gain strength in the early 20th century with the emergence of Pan-Africanism, as advocated by the Jamaican journalist <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Garvey" title="Marcus Garvey">Marcus Garvey</a> (1887–1940) whose widely distributed newspapers demanded swift abolition of European imperialism, as well as republicanism in Egypt. <a href="/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah" title="Kwame Nkrumah">Kwame Nkrumah</a> (1909–1972) who was inspired by the works of Garvey led <a href="/wiki/Ghana" title="Ghana">Ghana</a> to independence from colonial rule.
</p><p>Independence for the colonies in Africa began with the independence of <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a> in 1956, and <a href="/wiki/Ghana" title="Ghana">Ghana</a> in 1957. All of the British colonies on mainland Africa became independent by 1966, although <a href="/wiki/Rhodesia" title="Rhodesia">Rhodesia</a>'s unilateral declaration of independence in 1965 was not recognized by the UK or internationally.
</p><p>Some of the British colonies in Asia were directly administered by British officials, while others were ruled by local monarchs as <a href="/wiki/Protectorate" title="Protectorate">protectorates</a> or in <a href="/wiki/Subsidiary_alliance" title="Subsidiary alliance">subsidiary alliance</a> with the UK.
</p><p>In 1947, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_India" title="British India">British India</a> was <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_India" title="Partition of India">partitioned</a> into the independent dominions of <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>. Hundreds of <a href="/wiki/Princely_state" title="Princely state">princely states</a>, states ruled by monarchs in treaty of subsidiary alliance with Britain, were <a href="/wiki/Political_integration_of_India" title="Political integration of India">integrated into India</a> and Pakistan. India and Pakistan fought several wars over the former princely state of <a href="/wiki/Jammu_and_Kashmir_(princely_state)" title="Jammu and Kashmir (princely state)">Jammu and Kashmir</a>. <a href="/wiki/French_India" title="French India">French India</a> was integrated into India between 1950 and 1954, and India annexed <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_India" title="Portuguese India">Portuguese India</a> in 1961, and the Kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Sikkim" title="Sikkim">Sikkim</a> merged with India by popular vote in 1975.
</p>
<h4><span id="Violence.2C_civil_warfare.2C_and_partition"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Violence,_civil_warfare,_and_partition">Violence, civil warfare, and partition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decolonization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Violence, civil warfare, and partition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Surrender_of_Lord_Cornwallis.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1978" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Surrender_of_Lord_Cornwallis.jpg/220px-Surrender_of_Lord_Cornwallis.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Surrender_of_Lord_Cornwallis.jpg/330px-Surrender_of_Lord_Cornwallis.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Surrender_of_Lord_Cornwallis.jpg/440px-Surrender_of_Lord_Cornwallis.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Surrender_of_Lord_Cornwallis.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Surrender of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lord_Cornwallis" title="Lord Cornwallis">Lord Cornwallis</a> at <a href="/wiki/Yorktown,_Virginia" title="Yorktown, Virginia">Yorktown</a> in 1781</div></div></div>
<p>Significant violence was involved in several prominent cases of decolonization of the British Empire; partition was a frequent solution. In 1783, the North American colonies were divided between the independent United States, and <a href="/wiki/British_North_America" title="British North America">British North America</a>, which later became Canada.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Indian_Rebellion_of_1857" title="Indian Rebellion of 1857">Indian Rebellion of 1857</a> was a revolt of a portion of the Indian Army. It was characterized by massacres of civilians on both sides. It was not a movement for independence, however, and only a small part of India was involved. In the aftermath, the British pulled back from modernizing reforms of Indian society, and the level of organised violence under the <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">British Raj</a> was relatively small. Most of that was initiated by repressive British administrators, as in the <a href="/wiki/Amritsar#Jallianwala_Bagh_massacre" title="Amritsar">Amritsar massacre of 1919</a>, or the police assaults on the <a href="/wiki/Salt_March" title="Salt March">Salt March</a> of 1930.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Large-scale communal violence broke out between Muslims and Hindus and Muslims and Sikhs after the British left in 1947 in the newly independent <a href="/wiki/Dominion" title="Dominion">dominions</a> of India and Pakistan. Much later, in 1970, further communal violence broke out within Pakistan in the detached eastern part of East Bengal, which became independent as <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a> in 1971.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Cyprus_since_1878" title="History of Cyprus since 1878">Cyprus</a>, which came under full British control in 1914 from the Ottoman Empire, was culturally divided between the majority <a href="/wiki/Greek_Cypriots" title="Greek Cypriots">Greek element</a> (which demanded "<a href="/wiki/Enosis" title="Enosis">enosis</a>" or union with Greece) and the minority Turks. London for decades assumed it needed the island to defend the Suez Canal; but after the Suez crisis of 1956, that became a minor factor, and Greek violence became a more serious issue. Cyprus became an independent country in 1960, but ethnic violence escalated until 1974 when Turkey invaded and partitioned the island. Each side rewrote its own history, blaming the other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Mandatory_Palestine" title="Mandatory Palestine">Palestine</a> became a <a href="/wiki/Mandate_for_Palestine" title="Mandate for Palestine">British mandate</a> from the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a>, and during the war the British gained support from both sides by making promises both to the Arabs and the Jews. (See <a href="/wiki/Balfour_Declaration" title="Balfour Declaration">Balfour Declaration</a>). Decades of ethno—religious violence resulted. The British pulled out, after dividing the Mandate into <a href="/wiki/State_of_Palestine" title="State of Palestine">Palestine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jordan" title="Jordan">Jordan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="French_Empire">French Empire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decolonization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: French Empire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/French_colonial_empire" title="French colonial empire">French colonial empire</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:EmpireFrench.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1400" data-file-width="2753" decoding="async" height="112" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/EmpireFrench.png/220px-EmpireFrench.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/EmpireFrench.png/330px-EmpireFrench.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/EmpireFrench.png/440px-EmpireFrench.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:EmpireFrench.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of the first (light blue) and second (dark blue) French colonial empires.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LaGuerreAMadagascar.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="7976" data-file-width="5300" decoding="async" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/LaGuerreAMadagascar.jpg/170px-LaGuerreAMadagascar.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/LaGuerreAMadagascar.jpg/255px-LaGuerreAMadagascar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/LaGuerreAMadagascar.jpg/340px-LaGuerreAMadagascar.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LaGuerreAMadagascar.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>French poster about the "<a href="/wiki/Franco-Hova_Wars" title="Franco-Hova Wars">Madagascar War</a>"</div></div></div>
<p>After World War I, the colonized people were frustrated at France's failure to recognize the effort provided by the French colonies (resources, but more importantly colonial troops – the famous <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tirailleurs" title="Tirailleurs">tirailleurs</a></i>). Although in <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Mosque_of_Paris" title="Great Mosque of Paris">Great Mosque of Paris</a> was constructed as recognition of these efforts, the French state had no intention to allow <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Self-rule" title="Self-rule">self-rule</a>, let alone grant <a href="/wiki/Independence" title="Independence">independence</a> to the colonized people. Thus, <a href="/wiki/Nationalism" title="Nationalism">nationalism</a> in the colonies became stronger in between the two wars, leading to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ibn_%27Abd_al-Karim_al-Khattabi" title="Muhammad Ibn 'Abd al-Karim al-Khattabi">Abd el-Krim</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rif_War_(1920)" title="Rif War (1920)">Rif War</a> (1921–1925) in <a href="/wiki/History_of_Morocco" title="History of Morocco">Morocco</a> and to the creation of <a href="/wiki/Messali_Hadj" title="Messali Hadj">Messali Hadj</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Star_of_North_Africa" title="Star of North Africa">Star of North Africa</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nationalism_and_resistance_in_Algeria" title="Nationalism and resistance in Algeria">Algeria</a> in 1925. However, these movements would gain full potential only after World War II.
</p><p>After World War I, France administered the former Ottoman territories of <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a>, and the former German colonies of <a href="/wiki/French_Togoland" title="French Togoland">Togoland</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_Cameroons" title="French Cameroons">Cameroon</a>, as League of Nations mandates. Lebanon declared its independence in 1943, and Syria in 1945.
</p><p>Although France was ultimately a victor of World War II, Nazi Germany's occupation of France and its North African colonies during the war had disrupted colonial rule. On October 27, 1946, France adopted a new constitution creating the <a href="/wiki/French_Fourth_Republic" title="French Fourth Republic">Fourth Republic</a>, and substituted the <a href="/wiki/French_Union" title="French Union">French Union</a> for the colonial empire. However power over the colonies remained concentrated in France, and the power of local assemblies outside France was extremely limited. On the night of March 29, 1947, a <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Madagascar_revolt" title="Madagascar revolt">nationalist uprising</a> led the French government headed by <a href="/wiki/Paul_Ramadier" title="Paul Ramadier">Paul Ramadier</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Section_Fran%C3%A7aise_de_l%27Internationale_Ouvri%C3%A8re" title="Section Française de l'Internationale Ouvrière">Socialist</a>) to violent repression: one year of bitter fighting, 11,000–40,000 Malagasy died.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dien_Bien_Phu_1954_French_prisoners.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="787" data-file-width="1180" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Dien_Bien_Phu_1954_French_prisoners.jpg/220px-Dien_Bien_Phu_1954_French_prisoners.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Dien_Bien_Phu_1954_French_prisoners.jpg/330px-Dien_Bien_Phu_1954_French_prisoners.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Dien_Bien_Phu_1954_French_prisoners.jpg/440px-Dien_Bien_Phu_1954_French_prisoners.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dien_Bien_Phu_1954_French_prisoners.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Captured French soldiers from <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Dien_Bien_Phu" title="Battle of Dien Bien Phu">Điện Biên Phủ</a>, escorted by Vietnamese troops, 1954</div></div></div>
<p>In 1946, the states of <a href="/wiki/French_Indochina" title="French Indochina">French Indochina</a> withdrew from the French Union, leading to the <a href="/wiki/First_Indochina_War" title="First Indochina War">Indochina War</a> (1946–54). <a href="/wiki/Ho_Chi_Minh" title="Ho Chi Minh">Ho Chi Minh</a>, who had been a co-founder of the <a href="/wiki/French_Communist_Party" title="French Communist Party">French Communist Party</a> in 1920 and had founded the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vietminh" title="Vietminh">Vietminh</a> in 1941, declared independence from France, and led the armed resistance against France's reoccupation of Indochina. <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Laos" title="Laos">Laos</a> became independent in 1953, and the 1954 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geneva_Conference_(1954)" title="Geneva Conference (1954)">Geneva Accords</a> ended France's occupation of Indochina, leaving <a href="/wiki/North_Vietnam" title="North Vietnam">North Vietnam</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Vietnam" title="South Vietnam">South Vietnam</a> independent.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carte_anachronique_des_colonies_fran%C3%A7aises.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3886" data-file-width="7648" decoding="async" height="112" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Carte_anachronique_des_colonies_fran%C3%A7aises.png/220px-Carte_anachronique_des_colonies_fran%C3%A7aises.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Carte_anachronique_des_colonies_fran%C3%A7aises.png/330px-Carte_anachronique_des_colonies_fran%C3%A7aises.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Carte_anachronique_des_colonies_fran%C3%A7aises.png/440px-Carte_anachronique_des_colonies_fran%C3%A7aises.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carte_anachronique_des_colonies_fran%C3%A7aises.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of all possessions of the first and second French colonial empires.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1956, <a href="/wiki/History_of_Morocco" title="History of Morocco">Morocco</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Tunisia" title="History of Tunisia">Tunisia</a> gained their independence from France. In 1960, eight independent countries emerged from <a href="/wiki/French_West_Africa" title="French West Africa">French West Africa</a>, and five from <a href="/wiki/French_Equatorial_Africa" title="French Equatorial Africa">French Equatorial Africa</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Algerian_War_of_Independence" title="Algerian War of Independence">Algerian War of Independence</a> raged from 1954 to 1962. To this day, the Algerian war – officially called a "public order operation" until the 1990s – remains a trauma for both France and Algeria. Philosopher <a href="/wiki/Paul_Ric%C5%93ur" title="Paul Ricœur">Paul Ricœur</a> has spoken of the necessity of a "decolonisation of memory", starting with the recognition of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1961_Paris_massacre" title="1961 Paris massacre">1961 Paris massacre</a> during the Algerian war, and the decisive role of African and especially North African immigrant manpower in the <i><a href="/wiki/Trente_Glorieuses" title="Trente Glorieuses">Trente Glorieuses</a></i> post–World War II economic growth period. In the 1960s, due to economic needs for post-war reconstruction and rapid economic growth, French employers actively sought to recruit manpower from the colonies, explaining today's <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_France" title="Demographics of France">multiethnic population</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="After_1918">After 1918</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decolonization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: After 1918">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/New_Imperialism" title="New Imperialism">New Imperialism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Socialism_liberation.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="269" data-file-width="370" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/20/Socialism_liberation.jpg/200px-Socialism_liberation.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/20/Socialism_liberation.jpg/300px-Socialism_liberation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/2/20/Socialism_liberation.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Socialism_liberation.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Czechoslovak anti-colonialist propaganda poster: "Socialism opened the door of liberation for colonial nations."</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decolonization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/American_imperialism" title="American imperialism">American imperialism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_United_States_military_operations" title="Timeline of United States military operations">Timeline of United States military operations</a></div>
<p>A union of former colonies itself, the United States approached imperialism differently from the other Powers. Much of its energy and rapidly expanding population was directed westward across the North American continent against English and French claims,  the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">Spanish Empire</a>  and Mexico.  The <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">Native Americans were sent to reservations, often unwillingly</a>. With support from Britain, its <a href="/wiki/Monroe_Doctrine" title="Monroe Doctrine">Monroe Doctrine</a> reserved the Americas as its sphere of interest, prohibiting other states (particularly Spain) from recolonizing the newly independent polities of <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin America</a>. However, France, taking advantage of the American government's distraction during the Civil War, intervened militarily in Mexico and set up a French-protected monarchy.  Spain took the step to <a href="/wiki/Spanish_occupation_of_the_Dominican_Republic" title="Spanish occupation of the Dominican Republic">occupy the Dominican Republic and restore colonial rule</a>. The Union victory in the Civil War in 1865 forced both France and Spain to accede to American demands to evacuate those two countries.  America's only African colony, <a href="/wiki/Liberia" title="Liberia">Liberia</a>, was formed privately and achieved independence early; Washington unofficially protected it. By 1900 the US advocated an <a href="/wiki/Open_Door_Policy" title="Open Door Policy">Open Door Policy</a> and opposed the direct division of China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Manuel_L._Quezon_(November_1942).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4350" data-file-width="3382" decoding="async" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Manuel_L._Quezon_%28November_1942%29.jpg/170px-Manuel_L._Quezon_%28November_1942%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Manuel_L._Quezon_%28November_1942%29.jpg/255px-Manuel_L._Quezon_%28November_1942%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Manuel_L._Quezon_%28November_1942%29.jpg/340px-Manuel_L._Quezon_%28November_1942%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Manuel_L._Quezon_(November_1942).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manuel_L._Quez%C3%B3n" title="Manuel L. Quezón">Manuel L. Quezón</a>, the first president of the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_the_Philippines" title="Commonwealth of the Philippines">Commonwealth of the Philippines</a> (from 1935 to 1944)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TTPI-locatormap.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="225" data-file-width="326" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/TTPI-locatormap.png/220px-TTPI-locatormap.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/91/TTPI-locatormap.png 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TTPI-locatormap.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Trust_Territory_of_the_Pacific_Islands" title="Trust Territory of the Pacific Islands">Trust Territory of the Pacific Islands</a> in <a href="/wiki/Micronesia" title="Micronesia">Micronesia</a> administered by the United States from 1947 to 1986</div></div></div>
<p>After 1898 direct intervention expanded in Latin America. The United States purchased Alaska from the Russian Empire in 1867 and annexed Hawaii in 1898. Following the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish-American_war" title="Spanish-American war">Spanish-American war</a> in 1898, the US added most of Spain's remaining colonies: <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Guam" title="Guam">Guam</a>. Deciding not to annex Cuba outright, the U.S. established it as a <a href="/wiki/Client_state" title="Client state">client state</a> with obligations including the perpetual lease of <a href="/wiki/Guant%C3%A1namo_Bay" title="Guantánamo Bay">Guantánamo Bay</a> to the U.S. Navy. The attempt of the first governor to void the island's constitution and remain in power past the end of his term provoked a rebellion that provoked a reoccupation between 1906 and 1909, but this was again followed by devolution. Similarly, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/McKinley_administration" title="McKinley administration">McKinley administration</a>, despite prosecuting the <a href="/wiki/Philippine%E2%80%93American_War" title="Philippine–American War">Philippine–American War</a> against a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Republic_of_the_Philippines" title="First Republic of the Philippines">native republic</a>, set out that the <a href="/wiki/Territories_of_the_United_States#Former_unincorporated_territories_of_the_United_States_(incomplete)" title="Territories of the United States">Territory of the Philippine Islands</a> was eventually granted independence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>   In 1917, the US purchased the <a href="/wiki/Danish_West_Indies" title="Danish West Indies">Danish West Indies</a> (later renamed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_Virgin_Islands" title="US Virgin Islands">US Virgin Islands</a>) from <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a> and Puerto Ricans became full U.S. citizens that same year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> The US government declared Puerto Rico the territory was no longer a colony and stopped transmitting information about it to the United Nations Decolonization Committee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> As a result, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UN_General_Assembly_Resolution_748" title="UN General Assembly Resolution 748">UN General Assembly</a> removed Puerto Rico from the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_list_of_non-self-governing_territories" title="United Nations list of non-self-governing territories">U.N. list of non-self-governing territories</a>.  Four referenda showed little support for independence, but much interest in statehood such as Hawaii and Alaska received in 1959.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Monroe Doctrine was expanded by the <a href="/wiki/Roosevelt_Corollary" title="Roosevelt Corollary">Roosevelt Corollary</a> in 1904, providing that the United States had a right and obligation to intervene "in flagrant cases of such wrongdoing or impotence" that a nation in the Western Hemisphere became vulnerable to European control. In practice, this meant that the United States was led to act as a collections agent for European creditors by administering customs duties in the <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Republic" title="Dominican Republic">Dominican Republic</a> (1905–1941), <a href="/wiki/Haiti" title="Haiti">Haiti</a> (1915–1934), and elsewhere. The intrusiveness and bad relations this engendered were somewhat checked by the <a href="/wiki/Clark_Memorandum" title="Clark Memorandum">Clark Memorandum</a> and renounced by President <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a>'s "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Good_Neighbor_Policy" title="Good Neighbor Policy">Good Neighbor Policy</a>."
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Fourteen_Points" title="Fourteen Points">Fourteen Points</a> were preconditions addressed by President <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson" title="Woodrow Wilson">Woodrow Wilson</a> to the European powers at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paris_Peace_Conference,_1919%E2%80%931920" title="Paris Peace Conference, 1919–1920">Paris Peace Conference</a> following <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>.  In allowing allies France and Britain the former colonial possessions of the German and Ottoman Empires, the US demanded of them submission to the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations_mandate" title="League of Nations mandate">League of Nations mandate</a>, in calling for <i>V. A free, open-minded, and absolutely impartial adjustment of all colonial claims, based upon a strict observance of the principle that in determining all such questions of sovereignty <b>the interests of the populations concerned must have equal weight</b> with the equitable government whose title is to be determined.</i> See also point XII.
</p><p>After 1947, the U.S. poured tens of billions of dollars into the <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Plan" title="Marshall Plan">Marshall Plan</a>, and other grants and loans to Europe and Asia to rebuild the world economy. Washington pushed hard to accelerate decolonization and bring an end to the colonial empires of its Western allies, most importantly during the 1956 <a href="/wiki/Suez_Crisis" title="Suez Crisis">Suez Crisis</a>, but American military bases were established around the world and direct and indirect interventions continued in <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Indochina</a>, Latin America (<i>inter alia</i>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1965_United_States_occupation_of_the_Dominican_Republic" title="1965 United States occupation of the Dominican Republic">1965 occupation of the Dominican Republic</a>), Africa, and the Middle East to oppose Communist invasions and insurgencies. Since the dissolution of the Soviet Union, the United States has been far less active in the Americas, but invaded <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(2001%E2%80%93present)" title="War in Afghanistan (2001–present)">Afghanistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iraq_War" title="Iraq War">Iraq</a> following the <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">September 11 attacks</a> in 2001, establishing army and air bases in <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Japan">Japan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decolonization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Japan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:US_Army_in_Korea_under_Japanese_Rule.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="378" data-file-width="513" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/US_Army_in_Korea_under_Japanese_Rule.JPG/220px-US_Army_in_Korea_under_Japanese_Rule.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/US_Army_in_Korea_under_Japanese_Rule.JPG/330px-US_Army_in_Korea_under_Japanese_Rule.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/US_Army_in_Korea_under_Japanese_Rule.JPG/440px-US_Army_in_Korea_under_Japanese_Rule.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:US_Army_in_Korea_under_Japanese_Rule.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>U.S. troops in <a href="/wiki/Korea_under_Japanese_rule" title="Korea under Japanese rule">Korea</a>, September 1945</div></div></div>
<p>Before World War I, Japan had gained several substantial colonial possessions in East Asia such as Taiwan (1895) and Korea (1910). Japan joined the allies in World War I, and after the war acquired the <a href="/wiki/South_Seas_Mandate" title="South Seas Mandate">South Seas Mandate</a>, the former German colony in Micronesia, as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/League_of_Nations_Mandate" title="League of Nations Mandate">League of Nations Mandate</a>. Pursuing a colonial policy comparable to those of European powers, Japan settled significant populations of ethnic Japanese in its colonies while simultaneously suppressing Indigenous ethnic populations by enforcing the learning and use of the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese language</a> in schools. Other methods such as public interaction, and attempts to eradicate the use of <a href="/wiki/Korean_language" title="Korean language">Korean</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hokkien" title="Hokkien">Hokkien</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hakka_Chinese" title="Hakka Chinese">Hakka</a> among the Indigenous peoples, were seen to be used. Japan also set up the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Universities" title="Imperial Universities">Imperial Universities</a> in Korea (<a href="/wiki/Keij%C5%8D_Imperial_University" title="Keijō Imperial University">Keijō Imperial University</a>) and Taiwan (<a href="/wiki/National_Taiwan_University" title="National Taiwan University">Taihoku Imperial University</a>) to compel education.
</p><p>In 1931, Japan seized <a href="/wiki/Manchuria" title="Manchuria">Manchuria</a> from the Republic of China, setting up a puppet state under <a href="/wiki/Puyi" title="Puyi">Puyi</a>, the last Manchu emperor of China. In 1933 Japan seized the Chinese province of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jehol_Province" title="Jehol Province">Jehol</a>, and incorporated it into its Manchurian possessions. The <a href="/wiki/Second_Sino-Japanese_War" title="Second Sino-Japanese War">Second Sino-Japanese War</a> started in 1937, and Japan occupied much of eastern China, including the Republic's capital at <a href="/wiki/Nanjing" title="Nanjing">Nanjing</a>. An estimated 20 million Chinese died during the 1931–1945 war with Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>In December 1941, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_Empire" title="Japanese Empire">Japanese Empire</a> joined <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> by invading the European and US colonies in Southeast Asia and the Pacific, including <a href="/wiki/French_Indochina" title="French Indochina">French Indochina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a>, the Philippines, Burma, <a href="/wiki/British_Malaya" title="British Malaya">Malaya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Timor" title="Portuguese Timor">Portuguese Timor</a>, and others. Following its surrender to the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Allies</a> in 1945, Japan was deprived of all its colonies with a number of them being returned to the original colonizing Western powers. The <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soviet%E2%80%93Japanese_War_(1945)" title="Soviet–Japanese War (1945)">declared war on Japan in August 1945</a>, and shortly after occupied and annexed the southern <a href="/wiki/Kuril_Islands" title="Kuril Islands">Kuril Islands</a>, which Japan <a href="/wiki/Kuril_Islands_dispute" title="Kuril Islands dispute">still claims</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="After_1945">After 1945</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decolonization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: After 1945">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Planning_for_decolonization">Planning for decolonization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decolonization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Planning for decolonization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="U.S._and_Philippines">U.S. and Philippines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decolonization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: U.S. and Philippines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>In the United States, the two major parties were divided on the acquisition of the Philippines, which became a major campaign issue in 1900. The Republicans, who favored permanent acquisition, won the election, but after a decade or so, Republicans turned their attention to the Caribbean, focusing on building the <a href="/wiki/Panama_Canal" title="Panama Canal">Panama Canal</a>. President <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson" title="Woodrow Wilson">Woodrow Wilson</a>, a Democrat in office from 1913 to 1921, ignored the Philippines, and focused his attention on Mexico and Caribbean nations. By the 1920s, the peaceful efforts by the Filipino leadership to pursue independence proved convincing. When the Democrats returned to power in 1933, they worked with the Filipinos to plan a smooth transition to independence. It was scheduled for 1946 by <a href="/wiki/Tydings%E2%80%93McDuffie_Act" title="Tydings–McDuffie Act">Tydings–McDuffie Act</a> of 1934. In 1935, the Philippines transitioned out of territorial status, controlled by an appointed governor, to the semi-independent status of the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_the_Philippines" title="Commonwealth of the Philippines">Commonwealth of the Philippines</a>. Its constitutional convention wrote a new constitution, which was approved by Washington and went into effect, with an elected governor <a href="/wiki/Manuel_L._Quezon" title="Manuel L. Quezon">Manuel L. Quezon</a> and legislature. Foreign Affairs remained under American control. The Philippines built up a new army, under general <a href="/wiki/Douglas_MacArthur" title="Douglas MacArthur">Douglas MacArthur</a>, who took leave from his U.S. Army position to take command of the new army reporting to Quezon. The Japanese occupation 1942 to 1945 disrupted but did not delay the transition. It took place on schedule in 1946 as <a href="/wiki/Manuel_Roxas" title="Manuel Roxas">Manuel Roxas</a> took office as president.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Portugal">Portugal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decolonization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Portugal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:212px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sempreatentos...aoperigo!.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="437" data-file-width="511" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Sempreatentos...aoperigo%21.jpg/210px-Sempreatentos...aoperigo%21.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Sempreatentos...aoperigo%21.jpg/315px-Sempreatentos...aoperigo%21.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Sempreatentos...aoperigo%21.jpg/420px-Sempreatentos...aoperigo%21.jpg 2x" width="210"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sempreatentos...aoperigo!.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Army" title="Portuguese Army">Portuguese Army</a> special <i><a href="/wiki/Ca%C3%A7adores" title="Caçadores">caçadores</a></i> advancing in the African jungle in the early 1960s, during the <a href="/wiki/Angolan_War_of_Independence" title="Angolan War of Independence">Angolan War of Independence</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>As a result of its pioneering <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Portuguese_discoveries" title="Portuguese discoveries">discoveries</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a> had a large and particularly long-lasting colonial empire which had begun in 1415 with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conquest_of_Ceuta" title="Conquest of Ceuta">conquest of Ceuta</a> and ended only in 1999 with the handover of <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Macau" title="Portuguese Macau">Portuguese Macau</a> to China. In 1822, Portugal <a href="/wiki/Independence_of_Brazil" title="Independence of Brazil">lost control of Brazil</a>, its largest colony.
</p><p>From 1933 to 1974, <a href="/wiki/Estado_Novo_(Portugal)" title="Estado Novo (Portugal)">Portugal was an authoritarian state</a> (ruled by <a href="/wiki/Ant%C3%B3nio_de_Oliveira_Salazar" title="António de Oliveira Salazar">António de Oliveira Salazar</a>). The regime was fiercely determined to maintain the country's colonial possessions at all costs and to aggressively supress any insurgencies. In 1961, <a href="/wiki/Annexation_of_Goa" title="Annexation of Goa">India annexed Goa</a> and by the same year nationalist forces had begun organizing in Portugal. Revolts (preceding the <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Colonial_War" title="Portuguese Colonial War">Portuguese Colonial War</a>) spread to <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Angola" title="Portuguese Angola">Angola</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Guinea" title="Portuguese Guinea">Guinea Bissau</a> and <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Mozambique" title="Portuguese Mozambique">Mozambique</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lisbon" title="Lisbon">Lisbon</a> escalated its effort in the war: for instance, it increased the number of natives in the colonial army and built strategic hamlets. Portugal sent another 300,000 European settlers into Angola and Mozambique before 1974. That year, <a href="/wiki/Carnation_Revolution" title="Carnation Revolution">a left-wing revolution</a> inside Portugal overthrew the existing regime and encouraged pro-Soviet elements to attempt to seize control in the colonies. The result was a very long and extremely difficult multi-party <a href="/wiki/Angolan_Civil_War" title="Angolan Civil War">Civil War in Angola</a>, and lesser insurrections in Mozambique.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Belgium">Belgium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decolonization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Belgium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>Belgium's empire began with the annexation of the Congo in 1908 in response to international pressure to bring an end to the <a href="/wiki/Atrocities_in_the_Congo_Free_State" title="Atrocities in the Congo Free State">terrible atrocities</a> that had taken place under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_Leopold_II_of_Belgium" title="King Leopold II of Belgium">King Leopold</a>'s privately run <a href="/wiki/Congo_Free_State" title="Congo Free State">Congo Free State</a>. It added <a href="/wiki/Ruanda-Urundi" title="Ruanda-Urundi">Rwanda and Burundi</a> as League of Nations mandates from the former German Empire in 1919. The colonies remained independent during the war, while Belgium itself was occupied by the Germans. There was no serious planning for independence, and exceedingly little training or education provided.  The <a href="/wiki/Belgian_Congo" title="Belgian Congo">Belgian Congo</a> was especially rich, and many Belgian businessmen lobbied hard to maintain control. Local revolts grew in power and finally, the Belgian king suddenly announced in 1959 that independence was on the agenda – and it was hurriedly arranged in 1960, for country bitterly and deeply divided on social and economic grounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Netherlands">The Netherlands</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decolonization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: The Netherlands">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
